

Chapter Summaries
How Science Works
Ritchie opens not with high theory but plumbing—the surprisingly recent invention of peer review, which didn't become universal until the 1970s. The chapter traces how philosophical transactions evolved from 17th-century newsletters into today's ecosystem of 30,000 journals, establishing the social processes that give science its authority: peer review, publication, citation. But the framework immediately shows cracks. Robert Merton's four scientific norms—universalism, communalism, disinterestedness, organized skepticism—read less as description than aspiration. The chapter introduces its central tension: science depends on trust (that studies happened as reported, that numbers weren't fabricated) while simultaneously claiming not to require trust (nullius in verba—take nobody's word). This contradiction, Ritchie suggests, is where everything goes wrong. By chapter's end, the idealized peer review process looks less like quality control than collective self-deception.
The Replication Crisis
The numbers accumulate with sickening momentum. When researchers attempted to replicate 100 prominent psychology studies, only 39% succeeded. For papers in Nature and Science—journals representing science's supposed pinnacle—62% replicated, meaning more than one-third failed. In cancer research: 11% of landmark preclinical studies held up. Ritchie catalogs the wreckage: priming effects that vanish under scrutiny, power posing that doesn't replicate, the Stanford Prison Experiment revealed as stage-managed theater. The chapter's most disturbing implication isn't that individual studies fail—it's the realization that hardly anyone runs replication studies at all. One estimate put it at 1% of published research. The scientific literature, supposedly humanity's accumulating knowledge, turns out to be substantially fiction. Daniel Kahneman's confident assertion that readers have "no choice but to accept" priming effects—written by a Nobel laureate about research later debunked—becomes the chapter's dark emblem of unwarranted certainty.
Fraud
Paolo Macchiarini implanted synthetic tracheas into patients who died agonizing deaths, published glowing results in The Lancet, and convinced the Karolinska Institute to defend him until Swedish television made denial impossible. The chapter moves through a rogues' gallery—Woo Suk Hwang's faked stem cells, Haruko Obokata's doctored images, Yoshitaka Fujii's 183 fabricated papers—but Ritchie's real target is systemic. Elizabeth Bick's heroic manual review of 20,621 papers found 3.8% contained problematic images; projected across biology, that's 35,000 papers needing retraction. The question isn't whether fraud exists but why institutions protect fraudsters. The Karolinska initially exonerated Macchiarini, attacked whistleblowers, even reported them to police. Science's self-image as objective and honest, Ritchie argues, paradoxically prevents it from spotting villains in its midst. The chapter's devastating conclusion: surveyed scientists estimate 14% of their colleagues have falsified data, yet retractions remain rare. Trust becomes science's vulnerability rather than its strength.
Bias
Stephen J. Gould accused Samuel Morton of unconsciously massaging 19th-century skull measurements to support white supremacy—then later researchers accused Gould of the same bias in reverse. The chapter spirals through publication bias (98% of positive antidepressant trials published versus 48% of negative ones), p-hacking (Brian Wansink encouraging students to dig through data "until something becomes significant"), and outcome switching (68% of null medical trials containing "spin"). Ritchie explains how the arbitrary p<0.05 threshold combines with human confirmation bias to corrupt research at every stage. The garden of forking paths metaphor captures how analyzing data without pre-planned routes leads researchers toward statistical flukes they mistake for discoveries. Most disturbing: Dana Carney's frank admission that her famous power-posing study "surely was" p-hacked, met not with condemnation but applause for her honesty. The chapter reveals bias not as individual moral failing but as inevitable consequence of giving humans flexible analytical tools and career incentives favoring positive results.
Negligence
The Reinhart-Rogoff Excel error—five countries accidentally excluded from calculations—helped justify austerity policies across Europe and America. When corrected, their claimed relationship between high debt and economic contraction essentially disappeared. The paper had been cited over 3,000 times, explicitly referenced by politicians. Ritchie catalogs how basic mistakes pervade published research: StatCheck found nearly half of psychology papers contained numerical inconsistencies, 13% had errors potentially changing conclusions. The Grim test revealed that celebrated studies, including Festinger's foundational 1959 cognitive dissonance research, reported mathematically impossible averages. Cell line contamination has produced 32,755 papers using "impostor cells"—often from wrong species entirely—with the problem known since 1958 yet barely addressed. The chapter's most damning section covers statistical power: vast numbers of studies are too small to detect the effects they claim to find, producing a literature resembling the discredited candidate gene approach in genetics—impressive effects that evaporate under proper scrutiny.
Hype
NASA's 2010 press release about "arsenic life" claimed the definition of life had "just expanded," announcing bacteria that replaced DNA's phosphorus with arsenic. Within months, replications showed the bacteria only grew when phosphorus was provided; the arsenic was contamination. The chapter dissects how hype moves through the system: 40% of health press releases gave unwarranted advice, 36% made crosse-species leaps from mice to humans, 33% turned correlation into causation. When press releases exaggerated causal claims, news stories were 20 times more likely to follow suit. Books amplify the problem—Carol Dweck's "growth mindset" correlates with 1% of grade variation yet spawned an educational movement; Matthew Walker's sleep claims about cancer risk don't match his underlying studies. The microbiome becomes exhibit A for runaway hype: a Cell paper with 16 autistic children's fecal samples transplanted to mice, questionable statistics, buried null results, yet press release suggesting probiotics might "one day" treat autism. Scientists themselves, not just journalists, drive the hype cycle—and it feeds back into publication decisions, creating self-sustaining distortion.
Perverse Incentives
Chinese universities pay cash bonuses for publications—sometimes exceeding annual salary for papers in Nature or Science. But cash-for-papers merely makes explicit what operates implicitly everywhere: scientists need publications for jobs, tenure, grants; universities need high-impact papers for rankings and funding; journals need citations for impact factors. The result is Smaldino and McElreath's "natural selection of bad science"—systems rewarding those who game metrics over those prioritizing rigor. Ritchie documents the predictable responses: salami slicing (one geneticist publishing single studies as 23 separate papers, one per chromosome), predatory journals, fake peer review, self-citation cartels. One review article cited 445 papers from Cell Transplantation, all within two years—three of four authors sat on Cell Transplantation's editorial board. The chapter's computer models show how rewarding publication quantity over quality causes false discovery rates to skyrocket over time. Young evolutionary biologists hired in 2013 had nearly twice as many publications as those hired in 2005—annual escalation of productivity requirements selecting for corner-cutting rather than care.
Fixing Science
Technology offers detection: algorithms spotting impossible statistics, fake images, suspicious randomization. But Ritchie recognizes technical solutions address symptoms, not causes. Real reform requires changing incentives. Pre-registration (publicly committing to analysis plans before seeing data) dropped positive trial results from 57% to 8% after becoming mandatory. Registered reports—where journals commit to publishing based on methods rather than results—eliminate publication bias entirely. Open science makes fraud harder, errors easier to detect. But implementation lags: only 31% of medical meta-analyses checked for publication bias; scientists share data with colleagues only 26% of the time; many pre-registered trials still fail to report results. The chapter surveys proposals from modest (automated error-checking) to radical (grant lotteries, paying scientists rather than projects, separating peer review from publication). The common thread: rewarding transparency, rigor, and replication rather than novelty, impact factors, and sheer publication volume. Plan S's requirement that all European-funded research appear in open-access journals by 2021 shows coordinated action can force systemic change.
Epilogue
Ritchie confronts the obvious objection: won't exposing science's failures undermine public trust? His response inverts the concern—silence does more damage than transparency. The constant drumbeat of hyped breakthroughs followed by quiet retractions erodes trust more than honest reckoning. Climate change deniers already weaponize scientific uncertainty; pretending science is infallible makes eventual revelation more devastating. The epilogue insists that sophisticated trust in science isn't unquestioning faith but organized skepticism—nullius in verba, take nobody's word. When the US Department of Agriculture tried requiring "preliminary" labels on climate research (transparently political), some scientists overcompensated by claiming published papers are "finalized, nothing preliminary." This, Ritchie argues, mistakes science's idealized self-image for reality. The corruption of science carries real costs: patients who died from Macchiarini's tracheas, billions in research funding toward flashy hypotheses rather than rigorous groundwork, a literature so polluted that even scientists struggle to distinguish signal from noise. The path forward requires acknowledging that science's authority derives not from infallibility but from commitment to self-correction.

The chapters sketch a diagnosis more systematic than most practicing scientists want to acknowledge. What binds fraud, bias, negligence, and hype isn't individual moral failing but structural incentives rewarding the wrong things. The question Ritchie keeps circling back to—can science's self-correcting mechanisms still function?—grows more urgent with each section. By the book's end, the answer seems less certain than the question itself.

The Replication Crisis as Epistemological Reckoning
There's something almost quaint about the faith encoded in the phrase "peer review." It suggests a gathering of equals, mutual scrutiny, the best kind of collegial oversight. The reality Stuart Ritchie documents in Science Fictions is more unsettling: a system that didn't exist in its current form until the 1970s, that operates largely on trust it cannot verify, and that has proven remarkably ineffective at its ostensible purpose of filtering truth from error.
When I first encountered the replication crisis—not in Ritchie's book but through the slow accumulation of failed replications in my own reading of psychology papers—what struck me wasn't the scale of the problem but the peculiar silence surrounding it. Papers would fail to replicate, the failures would be published in specialty journals or relegated to footnotes, and the original papers would continue to be cited as if nothing had happened. It was like watching a building develop cracks while everyone inside carried on filing their usual paperwork.
Ritchie, a psychologist and metascientist at King's College London, has produced something rarer than it should be: a comprehensive, empirically grounded diagnosis of how modern science systematically produces unreliable knowledge. The book's structure mirrors its argument. Four categories of failure—fraud, bias, negligence, hype—build toward a single explanatory framework: perverse incentives built into academic science that reward publication volume and attention-grabbing results over methodological rigor. The progression isn't merely taxonomic; each chapter reveals how attempts to game one metric create pressure toward the next failure mode.
The numbers accumulate with compound-interest momentum. When researchers attempted to replicate 100 prominent psychology studies, only 39% succeeded. For papers in Nature and Science—journals supposedly representing the pinnacle of scientific rigor—the rate climbed to 62%, which means more than one-third still failed. In economics, 61% replication for microeconomic studies. In cancer research, a mere 11% of landmark preclinical studies held up under independent scrutiny. These aren't marginal findings from obscure journals; they're foundational papers that shaped fields and informed policy.
Consider the practical implications. You're a doctor prescribing antidepressants. The scientific literature presents overwhelmingly positive evidence—98% of positive trials published, compared to 48% of negative ones. After researchers corrected for publication bias and outcome switching, that 2-to-1 ratio collapsed. The drugs still work, but perhaps half as well as you believed. How many patients have suffered side effects for treatments whose efficacy was systematically exaggerated?
Or consider austerity policy. Reinhart and Rogoff's 2010 paper on debt-to-GDP ratios—cited by politicians including UK Chancellor George Osborne to justify spending cuts—contained an Excel error that excluded five countries from calculations. When corrected, the claimed relationship between high debt and economic contraction essentially disappeared. The paper had been cited over 3,000 times. A simple spreadsheet mistake, amplified through citation into policy affecting millions.
What distinguishes Ritchie's account from the usual laments about scientific fraud or methodological sloppiness is his refusal to locate the problem primarily in individual moral failing. Yes, fraudsters exist—Yoshitaka Fujii fabricating data for 183 papers, Paolo Macchiarini implanting synthetic tracheas that killed his patients. But they're statistical outliers. The real crisis lies in well-meaning researchers responding rationally to irrational incentives.
The incentive structure operates with elegant perversity. Universities rank departments by publication output and journal prestige. Funders distribute grants based on past publications. Journals compete for high impact factors by publishing attention-grabbing positive results. Scientists need publications to secure jobs, tenure, and funding. The result is what Paul Smaldino and Richard McElreath call "the natural selection of bad science"—systems that reward those who game metrics over those who prioritize rigor.
Consider Chinese universities paying cash bonuses for publications in prestigious journals, sometimes exceeding annual salary. The West's implicit incentives prove equally powerful. One analysis found that young evolutionary biologists hired in 2013 had nearly twice as many publications as those hired in 2005, suggesting annual escalation of productivity requirements. Under such pressure, what scientist wouldn't engage in "p-hacking"—running multiple analyses until random chance produces a statistically significant result below the sacred 0.05 threshold?
Brian Wansink, once-celebrated food psychology researcher with an Ig Nobel Prize and bestselling books, exemplifies the problem. A 2016 blog post inadvertently revealed his methods: encouraging a graduate student to analyze pizza restaurant data multiple times daily until "something" became statistically significant. When skeptics examined his papers, they found 150 errors in four studies, mislabeled graphs, misdescribed methodology. Eighteen papers have been retracted. Yet Wansink wasn't a fraud in the traditional sense—he was playing the game as the rules implicitly demanded it be played.
The p-hacking chapter contains the book's most sophisticated statistical explanations, and Ritchie navigates them with unusual clarity. The core insight: when you run multiple statistical tests on the same data, you give yourself multiple chances to find a false positive. If p<0.05 means 5% chance of error for one test, running five tests gives 23% chance of at least one false positive. Twenty tests: 64%. Scientists pursuing "statistically significant" results keep testing until they find them, then write papers as if they'd predicted the pattern in advance. The Texas sharpshooter fires randomly at a barn, then paints bullseyes around bullet clusters.
What makes this corruption of statistical practice particularly insidious is that it often occurs unconsciously. The "garden of forking paths" metaphor captures how seemingly innocent analytical decisions—do you exclude this outlier? combine these variables? adjust for this covariate?—multiply until researchers are effectively guaranteed to find something "significant" if they look hard enough. Even without intentional p-hacking, scientists who analyze data without pre-specified plans end up "overfitting"—finding patterns specific to their sample that won't replicate in others.
The book's most valuable contribution may be its refusal to treat these problems as mysteries requiring investigation. The mystery has been solved. We know why science is producing so much unreliable work: because that's what the incentive structure rewards. What remains is political and institutional—whether universities, funders, and journals will change systems that have calcified over decades.
Ritchie surveys reform movements with cautious optimism. Pre-registration, where scientists publicly commit to analysis plans before seeing data, has shown promise—one study found positive trial results dropped from 57% to 8% after registration became mandatory. Open data sharing makes fraud harder and errors easier to detect. "Registered reports," where journals commit to publishing studies based on methods rather than results, eliminate publication bias entirely. Plan S's requirement that all European-funded research appear in open-access journals by 2021 could reshape publishing economics.
Yet implementation lags disturbingly behind advocacy. Only 31% of medical meta-analyses check for publication bias. Scientists share data with colleagues only 26% of the time. Many pre-registered trials fail to report results. Technology offers solutions—algorithms detecting impossible statistics, fake images, suspicious randomization—but technology can't fix motivation.
The deepest question the book poses isn't about any specific reform but about scientific culture itself. Can a community that has spent decades optimizing for publication metrics and grant capture rediscover the Mertonian norms it claims to honor? Can universities stop evaluating researchers by publication counts and impact factors? Can funders distribute money based on rigor rather than novelty? Can journals prioritize methodological soundness over exciting results?
Reading Science Fictions alongside Ritchie's professional context adds resonance. As a working scientist documenting his field's failures, he writes from inside the system he critiques. The book emerged during psychology's most intense period of self-examination, when the field was simultaneously discovering its replication problems and developing solutions. This insider status gives the critique authority but also explains certain limits. The focus remains almost entirely on Anglo-American science, with developing world research infrastructure receiving minimal attention despite different incentive structures.
The book also underplays philosophical debates about what replication means. Failed replications don't automatically invalidate original findings—contexts differ, populations vary, measurement introduces noise. Some effects may be real but fragile, appearing only under specific conditions. The binary replicated/not-replicated framework risks oversimplifying scientific knowledge's provisional nature. When Ritchie dismisses stereotype threat research based on publication bias in its meta-analysis, he may be correct about the specific finding, but the move illustrates how replication-crisis rhetoric can occasionally overreach.
Still, these are quibbles about an essential book. Ritchie has produced the most comprehensive diagnosis of modern science's systemic failures, backed by meticulous documentation and clear-eyed analysis. His central argument—that perverse incentives corrupt research quality—deserves to reshape how universities, funders, and journals evaluate scientific work.
The question of public trust, which Ritchie addresses in the epilogue, deserves more attention than it often receives in these discussions. The standard worry is that exposing science's problems will undermine faith in scientific institutions. Ritchie's response inverts the concern: silence is more dangerous than transparency. The constant drumbeat of hyped breakthroughs followed by quiet retractions does more damage than honest reckoning. Climate change deniers already weaponize scientific uncertainty; pretending science is infallible only makes the eventual revelation more devastating.
This strikes me as correct but incomplete. The real challenge isn't maintaining blind trust in science but cultivating sophisticated skepticism—understanding that science is a process, not a product; that individual studies are provisional, not definitive; that the accumulation of evidence matters more than any single finding. The Royal Society's motto, nullius in verba (take nobody's word for it), captures science's founding principle: trust through verification, not authority.
What the replication crisis reveals is how far modern practice has drifted from that ideal. Scientists ask for public trust while hiding data, resisting scrutiny, and optimizing for metrics over truth. The corruption carries real human costs. Patients who received Macchiarini's synthetic tracheas endured agonizing deaths. Women were told to avoid peanuts during pregnancy based on studies later reversed, potentially increasing their children's allergy risk. Billions in research funding flow toward flashy hypotheses rather than rigorous groundwork.
Science Fictions makes an unfashionable argument: science needs more skepticism, not less. More transparency, more replication, more humble acknowledgment of uncertainty. The book's appendix on "How to Read a Scientific Paper" offers practical guidance—check sample sizes, look for pre-registration, examine effect sizes, search for independent replications—but its deeper message is philosophical.
In an era of misinformation and epistemic crisis, the temptation is to defend science by asserting its infallibility. Ritchie argues for the opposite: defending science by acknowledging its fallibility while demonstrating its commitment to self-correction. Science's authority derives not from being always right but from having systematic methods for discovering when it's wrong.
The path forward requires courage—to publish null results, to share data that might reveal errors, to prioritize rigor over novelty, to reward slow careful work in a culture celebrating rapid breakthroughs. It requires institutions willing to measure success by methodological quality rather than citation counts. It requires funders who recognize that most good science is incremental, most hypotheses are wrong, most experiments fail.
Above all, it requires acknowledging what the book makes impossible to ignore: science cannot simultaneously optimize for truth and for publication metrics. The choice will determine whether the next century of research builds on foundations we can trust, or collapses under the weight of its own unreliability.



The Broken Telescope: Inside Science's Replication Crisis
A Review of Stuart Ritchie's "Science Fictions"
You are a peer reviewer for one of the world's most prestigious journals. The manuscript before you claims that undergraduate students can see into the future—that exposing them to elderly-related words makes them walk more slowly down hallways, that showing them pornographic images before they appear on screen allows them to predict their location through psychic precognition. The study is methodologically sound, the statistics pass muster, the lead author is from an Ivy League institution. You approve it for publication.
The year is 2011. Within months, the study will fail to replicate. Within a decade, it will become exhibit A in what scientists now call the replication crisis—a systemic unraveling that reveals perhaps half of published research cannot be trusted.
Stuart Ritchie's "Science Fictions" arrives at a peculiar moment in the history of human knowledge: we have never known more about the universe, and we have never been more uncertain about what we know. A psychologist and metascientist at King's College London, Ritchie has compiled what amounts to a forensic audit of the scientific publication system, and his findings read less like academic critique than crime scene investigation.
The Arithmetic of Doubt
The numbers accumulate with the inexorable momentum of compound interest. When researchers attempted to replicate 100 prominent psychology studies, only 39% succeeded. For papers published in Nature and Science—journals that represent the supposed pinnacle of scientific rigor—the rate climbed to 62%, which is to say that more than one-third still failed. In economics, a 61% replication rate for microeconomic studies. In cancer research, a mere 11% of landmark preclinical studies held up under independent scrutiny.
Consider what this means in practice: you are a doctor prescribing antidepressants. The scientific literature presents you with overwhelmingly positive evidence—98% of positive trials published, compared to 48% of negative ones. After researchers corrected for publication bias and outcome switching, that 2-to-1 ratio collapsed. The drug still works, but perhaps half as well as you believed. How many of your patients have suffered side effects for treatments whose efficacy was systematically exaggerated?
The waste alone staggers: $28 billion spent annually in the United States on preclinical research that cannot be replicated. This figure excludes downstream costs—the failed clinical trials built on faulty foundations, the treatments rolled out to millions based on studies that crumble under examination.
The Architecture of Failure
Ritchie organizes his investigation around four cardinal sins: fraud, bias, negligence, and hype. Each receives its own anatomy, and what emerges is not a rogues' gallery of villains but a portrait of systemic dysfunction.
Fraud, the most lurid category, proves surprisingly common. The Japanese anesthesiologist Yoshitaka Fujii fabricated data for 183 papers—a world record—before anyone noticed his patient headache rates were statistically impossible. Italian surgeon Paolo Macchiarini implanted synthetic tracheas seeded with stem cells into patients, published glowing results in The Lancet, and watched as his patients died painful deaths. The Karolinska Institute, home of the Nobel Prize, defended him until Swedish television made further denial impossible.
But fraud is merely the visible symptom. Bias—the unconscious finagling that leads researchers to massage data until it yields publishable results—permeates research at every level. Ritchie documents the mechanics of "p-hacking," where scientists run multiple analyses and report only those that cross the arbitrary threshold of statistical significance. He traces publication bias, where null results vanish into file drawers while positive findings flood journals. He demonstrates outcome switching in clinical trials, where researchers quietly change their primary endpoints after seeing disappointing results.
One study found that 68% of medical trials with null results contained "spin" in their abstracts—language designed to distract from the absence of positive findings. Another revealed that brain imaging studies routinely inflated their effect sizes through flexible analysis. The psychologist Brian Wansink, once celebrated for research on portion sizes and eating behavior, saw 18 papers retracted after admitting he'd encouraged a graduate student to dig through data until "something" became statistically significant.
The Negligence Quotient
If bias corrupts interpretation, negligence corrupts execution. Ritchie's chapter on errors reads like a catalog of professional malpractice. Researchers who don't randomize properly—Carlisle's analysis of 5,087 trials found 5% had suspicious randomization patterns. Scientists who run studies with laughably inadequate sample sizes, chasing effects too subtle to detect. Cell biologists using contaminated cell lines, with one review finding that 32,755 papers used "imposter cells" from the wrong species or tissue type.
The Reinhart-Rogoff affair offers perhaps the most consequential example: a simple Excel error—five countries accidentally excluded from the calculation—helped justify austerity policies across Europe and the United States. When corrected, the claimed relationship between high debt and economic contraction essentially disappeared. The paper had been cited over 3,000 times and explicitly referenced by politicians including UK Chancellor George Osborne.
Statistical errors pervade the literature. When researchers ran automated checks on 30,000 psychology papers, nearly half contained numerical inconsistencies. Thirteen percent had serious mistakes that potentially changed conclusions. The "Grim test" revealed that celebrated studies, including the foundational 1959 research on cognitive dissonance, reported mathematically impossible averages.
The Hype Machine
Hype, Ritchie's fourth category, completes the cycle of dysfunction. Press releases inflate preliminary findings into medical breakthroughs. Scientists write books making sweeping claims from narrow evidence—Carol Dweck's "growth mindset" research, which correlates with just 1% of variation in grades but spawned an educational movement. Matthew Walker's sleep research, where claims about cancer risk and mortality don't match the underlying studies. Amy Cuddy's "power posing," which generated 73.5 million TED talk views before failing to replicate.
The microbiome has become science's latest hype frontier. A paper in Cell claimed that fecal transplants from autistic children to mice produced autism-like behaviors—based on 16 human donors, with questionable statistical analysis and a null result quietly buried. The study's press release suggested probiotics might "one day" treat autism, a claim with no evidentiary basis whatsoever. Such papers provide scientific cover for a multibillion-dollar probiotic industry selling products with minimal proven benefit.
The Incentive Catastrophe
Ritchie's analysis ultimately points to a single source: perverse incentives built into academic science. Universities rank departments by publication output and journal prestige. Funders distribute grants based on past publications. Journals compete for high impact factors by publishing attention-grabbing positive results. Scientists need publications to secure jobs, tenure, and funding.
The result is what Smaldino and McElreath call "the natural selection of bad science"—a system that rewards those who game metrics over those who prioritize rigor. Some Chinese universities pay cash bonuses for publications in prestigious journals, sometimes exceeding annual salary. But the West's implicit incentives prove equally powerful. One analysis found that young evolutionary biologists hired in 2013 had nearly twice as many publications as those hired in 2005, suggesting an annual escalation of productivity requirements.
The pressure manifests in surreal ways. Scientists slice single studies into multiple papers ("salami slicing") to pad CVs. They create fake peer reviewer identities to approve their own work. They cite themselves obsessively to boost h-indices. Journals form "citation cartels," agreeing to cross-reference papers to inflate impact factors. One review article in Medical Science Monitor cited 445 papers from Cell Transplantation, all published within two years—three of four authors sat on Cell Transplantation's editorial board.
The Technical Fix Fallacy
Ritchie surveys the reform movement with cautious optimism. Pre-registration, where scientists publicly commit to analysis plans before seeing data, has shown promise—one study found positive trial results dropped from 57% to 8% after registration became mandatory. Open data sharing makes fraud harder and errors easier to detect. "Registered reports," where journals commit to publishing studies based on methods rather than results, eliminate publication bias entirely.
Technology offers new tools: algorithms to detect impossible statistics, fake images, and suspicious randomization patterns. Automated paper-writing software that populates results directly from data, eliminating transcription errors. Multiverse analysis, examining all defensible ways to analyze data rather than cherry-picking one.
But Ritchie recognizes that technical solutions address symptoms, not causes. The real question is whether the scientific community can reform incentive structures that have calcified over decades. Can universities stop evaluating researchers by publication counts and impact factors? Can funders distribute money based on rigor rather than novelty? Can journals prioritize methodological soundness over exciting results?


The Stakes of Truth
You might wonder whether publicizing science's failings undermines public trust. Ritchie argues the opposite: silence is more dangerous than transparency. The constant drumbeat of overhyped breakthroughs followed by quiet retractions does more damage than honest reckoning. Climate change deniers already weaponize scientific uncertainty; pretending science is infallible only makes the eventual revelation more devastating.
Moreover, the corruption of science carries real human costs. Patients who received Macchiarini's synthetic tracheas endured agonizing deaths. Women were told to avoid peanuts during pregnancy based on studies later reversed, potentially increasing their children's allergy risk. Billions in research funding flow toward flashy hypotheses rather than rigorous groundwork. The cumulative effect is a literature so polluted with unreliable findings that even scientists struggle to distinguish signal from noise.
The Royal Society's motto—Nullius in verba, "take nobody's word for it"—captures science's founding principle: trust through verification, not authority. The replication crisis reveals how far modern practice has drifted from that ideal. Scientists ask for public trust while hiding data, resisting scrutiny, and optimizing for metrics over truth.
"Science Fictions" makes an unfashionable argument: science needs more skepticism, not less. More transparency, more replication, more humble acknowledgment of uncertainty. The book's appendix on "How to Read a Scientific Paper" offers practical guidance—check sample sizes, look for pre-registration, examine effect sizes, search for independent replications—but its deeper message is philosophical.
The Sociology of Knowledge
What distinguishes Ritchie's account is his refusal to locate the problem in individual moral failing. Yes, fraudsters exist—Fujii, Stapel, Macchiarini—but they're statistical outliers. The real crisis lies in well-meaning researchers responding rationally to irrational incentives. When your career depends on publications, when journals reject null results, when statistical significance determines whether findings see daylight, p-hacking becomes not deviance but adaptation.
The book's most disturbing implication is that science's self-correcting mechanisms have broken down. Peer review, designed to filter out flawed work, instead rewards flashy claims. Replication, supposedly central to scientific validation, happens so rarely that one estimate put it at 1% of published research. The communalism that's meant to ensure data sharing faces researchers who refuse to provide data even to legitimate requests. Organized skepticism gives way to organized credibility, where the mere fact of publication in a prestigious journal confers automatic legitimacy.
Charles Babbage identified the same problems in 1830, in his "Reflections on the Decline of Science in England." He cataloged "hoaxing" (deliberate fakes later revealed), "forging" (fraud with no intention of disclosure), "trimming" and "cooking" (both forms of data manipulation). The publication system exacerbates these perennial human failings rather than containing them.
The Way Forward
Ritchie closes with genuine hope, grounded in evidence that reform is possible. The genetics field abandoned "candidate gene" studies after large-scale genome-wide association studies revealed they'd been chasing noise. Journals increasingly publish registered reports and require data sharing. Funders experiment with lotteries among qualified proposals, eliminating the grant-writing arms race. The COVID-19 pandemic demonstrated preprints' power, accelerating research by months or years through instant sharing.
The European funding consortium's "Plan S" demands that all supported research appear in fully open-access journals by 2021—a revolutionary intervention that could reshape publishing economics. Universities begin evaluating researchers on methodological rigor rather than publication counts. Grassroots movements like the UK Reproducibility Network push institutional change from below.
Whether these reforms gain traction depends on collective will. Science operates through social processes—peer review, publication, citation—and those processes respond to incentives. Change the incentives, change the outcomes. The alternative is continuing to pour billions into a system where half of published findings cannot be trusted, where medical treatments rest on quicksand, where scientific authority erodes through self-inflicted wounds.
The Reviewers' Verdict
"Science Fictions" achieves something rare: a deeply researched indictment that never loses sight of science's essential value. Ritchie writes as an insider, a practicing scientist who has published studies that failed to replicate, who understands the pressures that lead good researchers astray. His prose balances accessibility with precision, explaining statistical concepts without condescension while never dumbing down.
The book's structure—organizing problems into fraud, bias, negligence, and hype before exploring systemic causes—provides conceptual clarity. But it occasionally risks flattening complexity. Fraud and negligence are straightforward; bias operates at the boundary of consciousness; hype reflects publication pressures and media dynamics. Collapsing these into parallel categories obscures their different etiologies.
More significant: Ritchie focuses almost entirely on Anglo-American science, with occasional forays into China, Japan, and Europe. The developing world's research infrastructure receives minimal attention, despite different incentive structures and publication cultures. A truly global account might reveal whether these problems intensify or diminish in contexts with different academic traditions.
The book also underplays philosophy of science debates about what replication means. Failed replications don't automatically invalidate original findings—contexts differ, populations vary, measurement introduces noise. Some effects may be real but fragile, appearing only under specific conditions. The binary replicated/not-replicated framework risks oversimplifying scientific knowledge's provisional nature.
Still, these are quibbles about an essential book. Ritchie has produced the most comprehensive diagnosis of modern science's systemic failures, backed by meticulous documentation and clear-eyed analysis. His central argument—that perverse incentives corrupt research quality—deserves to reshape how universities, funders, and journals evaluate scientific work.
The Verdict
You sit in a faculty meeting, reviewing hiring candidates. One has 47 publications in high-impact journals, glittering metrics, a CV that screams success. Another has 12 papers, all pre-registered, all with open data, several null results honestly reported. Under current incentives, you know which candidate gets the job. Under the reforms Ritchie advocates, the question becomes more difficult—and more important.
"Science Fictions" forces a reckoning: science cannot simultaneously optimize for truth and for publication metrics. The choice will determine whether the next century of research builds on foundations we can trust, or collapses under the weight of its own unreliability. The book's greatest contribution is making that choice impossible to ignore.
For readers seeking to understand why so many celebrated findings fail to replicate, why medical advice reverses every few years, why nutrition science resembles alchemy more than chemistry, Ritchie provides answers grounded in systems analysis rather than individual blame. The problem isn't that scientists are dishonest—it's that honesty has become professionally costly, while corner-cutting pays dividends.
The path forward requires courage: to publish null results, to share data that might reveal errors, to prioritize rigor over novelty, to reward slow careful work in a culture that celebrates rapid breakthroughs. It requires institutions willing to measure success by methodological quality rather than citation counts. It requires funders who recognize that most good science is incremental, most hypotheses are wrong, most experiments fail.
Above all, it requires acknowledging that science's authority derives not from its infallibility but from its commitment to transparency and self-correction. "Science Fictions" is that commitment made manifest—a scientist exposing his field's failures not to destroy trust, but to earn it. In an era of misinformation and epistemic crisis, such honesty may be science's most valuable contribution.
Rating: ★★★★★ (5/5)
Recommended for: Scientists, policymakers, science journalists, anyone who relies on research to make decisions, readers interested in institutional reform, those concerned about research funding, students entering scientific careers.
Not recommended for: Those seeking simple narratives about heroic scientists or definitive medical advice; readers uncomfortable with statistical discussions; those who prefer triumphalist accounts of scientific progress.

Stuart Ritchie's "Science Fictions: How Fraud, Bias, Negligence, and Hype Undermine the Search for Truth" is published by Metropolitan Books/Henry Holt. 368 pages

## Science Fictions_ How Fraud, Bias, Negligence, and Hype Undermine the Search for - 0001 - Chapter 01

This is Audible. McMillan Audio presents Science Fictions, Written and Read by Stuart Richie.

## Science Fictions_ How Fraud, Bias, Negligence, and Hype Undermine the Search for - 0002 - Chapter 02

Now that is scientific fact. There's no real evidence for it, but it is scientific fact. Rassai Prefess It is the peculiar and perpetual error of the human understanding to be more moved and excited by affirmatives than by negatives. Francis Bacon, Novamorganum 1620 January 31, 2011, was the day the world found out that undergraduate students have psychic powers. A new scientific paper had hit the headlines. A set of laboratory experiments on over a thousand people had found evidence for psychic precognition, the ability to see into the future using extra sensory perception. This wasn't the work of some unknown crackpot. The paper was written by a top psychology professor, Darryl Bem, from the Ivy League's Cornell University. And it didn't appear in an obscure outlet. It was published in one of the most highly regarded mainstream peer-reviewed psychology journals. Science seemed to have given its official approval to a phenomenon that hitherto had been considered completely impossible. At the time, I was a PhD student studying psychology at the University of Edinburgh. I'd jutefully read Bem's paper. Here's how one of the experiments worked. Under graduate students looked at a computer screen, where two images of curtains would appear. They were told that there was another picture behind one of the curtains and that they had to click whichever they thought it was. Since they had no other information, they could only guess. After they'd chosen, the curtain disappeared and they saw whether they'd been correct. This was repeated 36 times, then the experiment was over. The results were quietly stunning. When a picture of some neutral boring object like a chair was behind one of the curtains, the outcome was almost perfectly random. The students chose correctly 49.8% of the time, essentially 50-50. However, and here's where it gets strange, when one of the pictures was pornographic, the students tended to choose it slightly more often than chance, 53.1% of the time to be exact. This met the threshold for statistical significance. In his paper, Bem suggested that some unconscious, evolved psychic sexual desire and ever so slightly nunch the students towards the erotic picture, even before it appeared on screen. Some of Bem's other experiments were less explicit but no less puzzling. In one of them, a list of 40 unrelated words appeared on the screen one at a time. Afterwards came a surprise memory test, where the students had to type in as many of the words as they could remember. At that point, the computer randomly selected 20 of the words and showed them to the students again. Then the experiment ended. Bem reported that during the memory test, the students were more likely to remember the 20 words they were about to see again, even though they couldn't have known except by psychic intuition, which ones they were going to be shown. This would be a bit like studying for an exam, sitting the exam, then studying again afterwards, and that post-exam study somehow winding its way back in time to improve your grade. Unless the laws of physics had suddenly been repealed, time is supposed to run in only one direction, causes are supposed to come before, not after their effects. But with the publication of Bem's paper, these bizarre results were now a part of the scientific literature. Crucially, Bem's experiments were extremely simple, requiring nothing more complicated than a desktop computer. If Bem was right, any researcher could produce evidence for the paranormal just by following his experimental instructions, even a PhD student with mixed and no resources. That is why I was, so that is exactly what I did. I got in touch with two other psychologists who were also skeptical of the results, Richard Weisman of the University of Hartfordshire and Chris French of Goldsmiths University of London. We agreed to rerun Bem's word list experiment three times, once at each of our respective universities. After a few weeks of recruiting participants, waiting for them to complete the memory test, and then dealing with the looks of bewilderment as we explained afterwards what we'd been looking for, we had the results. They showed nothing. Our undergraduates were in psychic. There was no difference in their recall of the words presented after the test. Perhaps the laws of physics were safe after all. We'd surely wrote up our results and sent the resulting paper off to the same scientific journal that had published Bem's study, the Journal of Personality and Social Psychology. Almost immediately, the door was slammed in our faces. The editor rejected the paper within a few days, explaining to us that they had a policy of never publishing studies that repeated a previous experiment, whether or not those studies found the same results as the original. Were we wrong to feel aggrieved? The journal had published a paper that had made some extremely bold claims. Claims that, if true, weren't just interesting to psychologists, but would completely revolutionize science. The results had made their way into the public domain, and received significant publicity in the popular media, including an appearance by Bem on the late night talk show The Colbert Report, where the hosts coined the memorable phrase, Time Traveling porn. Yet the editors wouldn't even consider publishing a replication study that called the findings into question. Meanwhile, another case was unfolding that also raised alarming questions about the current state of scientific practice. Science, widely considered one of the world's most prestigious scientific journals, second only to Nature, had published a paper by D. Derick Stappel, a social psychologist at Tilburg University in the Netherlands. The paper, entitled Coping with Chaos, described several studies performed in the lab and on the street, finding that people showed more prejudice and endorsed more racial stereotypes when in a messier or dirtier environment. This, and some of Stappel's dozens of other papers, hit the headlines across the world. Chaos promotes stereotyping, wrote Nature's news service. Where there's rubbish, there's racism, illiterated the Sydney Morning Herald. The results exemplified a type of social psychology research that produced easy to grasp findings with, as Stappel himself wrote, clear policy implications. In this case, to diagnose environmental disorder early and intervene immediately. The problem was that none of it was real. Some of Stappel's colleagues became suspicious. After they noticed the results of his experiments were a little too perfect. Not only that, but whereas senior academics are normally extremely busy and rely on their students to do such menial tasks as collecting data, Stappel had apparently gone out and collected all the data himself. After the colleagues brought these concerns to the university in September 2011, Stappel was suspended from his professorship. Multiple investigations followed. In a confessional autobiography he wrote subsequently, Stappel admitted that instead of collecting the data for his studies, he would sit alone in his office or at his kitchen table, late into the night, typing the numbers he required for his imaginary results into a spreadsheet, making them all up from scratch. I did some things that were terrible, maybe even disgusting, he wrote. I faked research data and invented studies that had never happened. I worked alone knowing exactly what I was doing. I didn't feel anything, no disgust, no shame, no regrets. His scientific fraud was surprisingly elaborate. I invented entire schools where I'd done my research, teachers with whom I'd discussed the experiments, lectures that I'd given, social studies lessons that I'd contributed to, gifts that I'd handed out as thanks for people's participation. Stappel described printing off the blank worksheets he'd ostensibly be giving to his participants, showing them to his colleagues and students announcing he was heading off to run the study, then dumping the sheets into the recycling when nobody was looking. It couldn't last. The findings of the investigations were clear, he was fired not long after his suspension. Since then, no fewer than 58 of his studies have been retracted, struck off the scientific record due to their fake data. The BEM and Stappel cases where esteemed professors published seemingly impossible results in BEM's case and outright fraudulent ones in Stappels sent a jolt through psychology research and through science more generally. How could prestigious scientific journals have allowed their publication? How many other studies had been published that couldn't be trusted? It turned out that these cases were perfect examples of much wider problems with the way we do science. In both cases, the central issue had to do with replication. For a scientific finding to be worth taking seriously, it can be something that occurred because of random chance or a glitch in the equipment or because the scientist was cheating or dissembling. It has to have really happened. And if it did, then in principle, I should be able to go out and find broadly the same results as yours. In many ways, that's the essence of science and something that sets it apart from other ways of knowing about the world. If it won't replicate, then it's hard to describe what you've done as scientific at all. What was concerning, then, wasn't so much that BEM's experiments were unreliable or that Stappels were a figment of his imagination. Some missteps and superior results will always do with us, and so alas, will fraudsters. What was truly problematic was how the scientific community had handled both situations. Our attempted replication of BEM's experiment was unceremoniously rejected from the journal that published the original. In the case of Stappel, nobody had ever even tried to replicate his findings. In other words, the community had demonstrated that it was content to take the dramatic claims in these studies at face value without checking how durable the results really were. And if there are no double checks on the replicability of results, how do we know they aren't just flukes or fakes? Perhaps BEM himself best summed up many scientists' attitudes to replication in an interview some years after his infamous study. I'm all for rigor, he said, but I don't have the patience for it. If you look to all my past experiments, they were always rhetorical devices. I gathered data to show how my point would be made. I used data as a point of persuasion, and I never really worried about will this replicate or will this not. Worrying about whether results will replicate or not isn't optional. It's the basic spirit of science, a spirit that's supposed to be made manifest in the system of peer review and journal publication, which acts as a bulwark against false findings, mistaken experiments, and dodgy data. As this book will show though, that system is badly broken. Important knowledge, discovered by scientists, but not deemed interesting enough to publish, is being altered or hidden, distorting the scientific record and damaging our medicine, technology, educational interventions, and government policies. Huge resources poured into science and the expectation of a useful return are being wasted on research that's utterly uninformative. Entirely avoidable errors and slip-ups routinely make it past the magi-no-line of peer review. Books, media reports, and our heads are being filled with facts that are either incorrect, exaggerated, or drastically misleading. And in the very worst cases, particularly where medical science is concerned, people are dying. Other books feature scientists taking the fight to a rogue's gallery of pseudo-scientists, creationists, homopaths, flat-airthers, astrologers, and the rilk, who misunderstand and abuse science, usually unwittingly, sometimes maliciously, and always irresponsibly. This book is different. It reveals a deep corruption within science itself, a corruption that affects the very culture in which research is practiced and published. Science, the discipline in which we should find the harshest skepticism, the most pin-sharp rationality, and the hardest-headed empiricism, has become home to a dizzying array of incompetence, delusion, lies, and self-deception. In the process, the central purpose of science to find our way ever closer to the truth is being undermined. The book begins by showing, in part one, that doing science involves much more than just running experiments or testing hypotheses. Science is inherently a social thing, where you have to convince other people, other scientists, of what you found. And since science is also a human thing, we know that any scientists will be prone to human characteristics, such as irrationality, biases, lapses in attention, in group-favoritism, and outright cheating to get what they want. To enable scientists to convince one another while trying to transcend the inherent limitations of human nature, science has evolved a system of checks and balances that, in theory, sorts the scientific wheat from the chaff. This process of scrutiny and validation, which leads to the supposed gold standard of publication in a peer-reviewed scientific journal, is described in chapter 1. But chapter 2 shows that the process must have gone terribly wrong. There are numerous published findings across many different areas of science that can't be replicated, and whose truth is very much in doubt. Then, in part 2, we'll ask why. We'll discover that our publication system, far from neutralizing or overriding all these human problems, allows them to leave their mark on the scientific record. And does so precisely because it believes itself to be objective and unbiased. A peculiar complacency, a strange arrogance, has taken hold, where the mere existence of the peer-review system seems to have stopped us from recognizing its flaws. Peer-reviewed papers are supposedly as near as one can get to an objective factual account of how the world works. But in our tour through many dozens of those papers, we'll discover that peer-review can't be relied upon to ensure that scientists are honest, chapter 3, detached, chapter 4, scriptulous, chapter 5, or sober, chapter 6, about their results. Part 3 digs deeper into scientific practice. Chapter 7 shows that it's not just that the system fails to deal with all the kinds of malpractice we've discussed. In fact, the way academic research is currently set up incentivizes these problems, encouraging researchers to obsess about prestige, fame, funding, and reputation at the expense of rigorous, reliable results. Finally, after we've diagnosed the problem, chapter 8 describes a set of often radical reforms to scientific practice that could help reorient it towards its original purpose, discovering facts about the world. To make the case about the frailties of scientific research, throughout the book I'll draw on cautionary tales from a wide variety of scientific fields. Partly because I'm a psychologist that we are preponderance of examples from that subject. My background isn't the only reason there's so much psychology in the book. It's also because after the BEM and staple affairs, among many others, psychologists have begun to engage in some intense soul-searching. More than perhaps any other field, we've begun to recognize our deep-seated flaws and to develop systematic ways to address them, ways that are beginning to be adopted across many different disciplines of science. The first step in fixing our broken scientific system is learning to spot and correct the mistakes that can be data-stray. And the only way to do this is with more science. Throughout the book, I'll draw on meta-science, a relatively new kind of scientific research that focuses on scientific research itself. If science is the process of exposing and eliminating errors, meta-science represents that process aimed inwards. Much can be learned from mistakes. On one of his albums, The musician Todd Rungren has a spoken word introduction encouraging the listener to play a little game he calls Sounds of the Studio. Rungren describes all the missteps that can be made when recording music. Hums, Hisses, pops on the microphone whenever someone sings a word with a P in it, choppy editing and so on. He suggests listening out for these mistakes in the songs that follow and on other records. And just as a better understanding of recording studio slip-ups can give you a new insight into how music is made, learning about how science goes wrong can tell you a lot about the process by which we arrive at our knowledge. Discovering the serious problems with the way we do science will be disconcerting. How many intriguing results that you've read about in the news and popular science books or seen in documentaries? Discoverers you've been excited enough to share with friends or that made you rethink how the world works are based on weak research that can't be replicated. How many times as your doctor prescribed you a drug or other treatment that rests on flawed evidence? How many times have you changed your diet, your purchasing habits or some other aspect of your lifestyle? On the basis of a scientific study, only for the evidence to be completely overturned by a new study a few months later. How many times have politicians made laws or policies that directly impact people's lives, citing science that won't stand up to scrutiny? In each case the answer is a lot more than you'd like to think. It's naive to hope that every single scientific study will be true. That is, a report of iron-clad facts that will never be overturned in future research. The world is far too messy a place for that. All we can hope for is that our scientific studies are trustworthy, that they honestly report what occurred in the research. If the much-vonted peer-review process can't justify that trust, science loses one of its most basic and most desirable qualities, along with its ability to do what it does best, revolutionize our world with a steady progression of new discoveries, technologies, treatments and cures. I come to praise science, not to bury it, this book is anything but an attack on science itself or on its methods. Rather, it is a defense of those methods and of scientific principles more generally against the way science is currently practiced. What makes all the disasters we'll encounter so disturbing is the importance of science. By allowing it to become so tarnished and its progress to be so badly stalled, we're in danger of ruining one of the greatest accomplishments of our species. But the damage isn't irreparable. In principle, if not in practice, science still has the potential to be the robust and reliable system of knowledge we need it to be. As we explore the litany of scientific failures in the book, the positive thought to hold on to the fragile scrap of hope and reassurance that it merges from the Pandora's box of fraud, bias, negligence and hype that will prize open in what follows is that nearly all of these problems have been uncovered by other scientists. The cliver met a scientific ideas that have been proposed to combat these problems and clear up the mess that has been created have come in substantial part from within the scientific community. Even if it's been deeply buried in many fields, the self-critical spirit that animates genuine science remains. And that's just as well because as we're about to find out, there really is quite a mess.

## Science Fictions_ How Fraud, Bias, Negligence, and Hype Undermine the Search for - 0003 - Chapter 03

Part 1. Ought and is Chapter 1. How Science Works Such subjects of thought furnish not sufficient employment in solitude, but require the company and conversation of our fellow creatures to render them a proper exercise for the mind. David Hume of essay writing, 1777. Science is a social construct. Before that statement makes you turn off the recording, let me explain what I mean. I don't mean it in the sense used by extreme relativists, postmodernists, anti-science crusaders, and others who suggest that there's no real world out there that science is only one not particularly special way of knowing about it, or even that science is just one myth among many that we could choose to believe. Science's cure diseases, map the brain, forecasted the climate, and split the atom. It's the best method we have of figuring out how the universe works and of bending it to our will. It is, in other words, our best way of moving towards the truth. Of course, we might never fully get there, a glanset history shows how hebristic it is to claim any facts as absolute or unchanging. For ratcheting our way towards better knowledge about the world though, the methods of science are as good as it gets. But we can't make progress with those methods alone. It's not enough to make a solitary observation in your lab. You must also convince other scientists that you've discovered something real. This is where the social part comes in. Philosophers have long discussed how important it is for scientists to show their fellow researchers how they came to their conclusions. John Stuart Mill puts it this way. In natural philosophy, there is always some other explanation possible of the same facts, some geocentric theory instead of heliocentric, some flogiston instead of oxygen. And it has to be shown why that other theory cannot be the true one. And until this is shown, and until we know how it is shown, we do not understand the grounds of our opinion. And so, scientists work together in teams, travel the world to give lectures and conference speeches, debate each other in seminars, form scientific societies to share research, and perhaps most importantly, publish the results in peer-reviewed journals. These social aspects aren't just a perk of the job or mere camaraderie. They're the process of science in action, an ongoing march of collective scrutiny, questioning, revision, refinement, and consensus. Although it might sound paradoxical at first, the subjective process of science is what provides it with its unmatched degree of objectivity. It's in this sense, then, that science is a social construct. Any claim about the world can only be described as scientific knowledge after it's been through this communal process, which is designed to sieve out errors and faults and allow other scientists to say whether they judge a new finding to be reliable, robust, and important. That each discovery has to run such a gauntlet, imbues the eventual products of the scientific process, the published peer-reviewed studies, with a great deal of power in society. This is no mere Kant, rhetoric, or opinion, we say. This is science. Science's social nature does come with weaknesses, however. The Cus scientists focus so much on trying to persuade their peers, which is the way they get those studies through peer-review and onward to publication, it's all too easy for them to disregard the real objective science, getting us closer to the truth. And because scientists are human beings, the ways that they try to persuade each other aren't always fully rational or objective. If we don't take great care, our scientific process can become permeated by very human flaws. This book is about how we haven't taken enough care of our precious scientific process. It's about how we ended up with a scientific system that doesn't just overlook our human foibles, but amplifies them. In recent years, it's become increasingly painfully obvious that peer-review is far from the guarantee of accuracy and reliability its cracked up to be, while the system of publication that's supposed to be a crucial strength of science has become its Achilles heel. To understand how the scientific publication system has gone so wrong, though, we first need to know how it's supposed to work when it goes right. Let's imagine you want to do some science. The first step is to read the scientific literature. The scientists insist of a vast library of journals, the specialist magazines that are the main outlets for new scientific knowledge. The idea of a periodical where scientists could share their work dates back to 1665, when Henry Oldenburg of the UK's Royal Society published the first issue of, to give it its full title, philosophical transactions, giving some a compt of the present undertaking studies and labours of the ingenious in many considerable parts of the world. The intention was that those ingenious scientists could send in letters describing their exploits for the perusal of other interested readers. Before that, scientists either laboured a loan in the courts of wealthy rulers or for private patrons or guilds, where their science was often seen as more akin to a parlor trick than an effort to discover the truth, published standalone books or formed letter writing circles with like-minded peers. Indeed, this latter kind of correspondence club is where institutions like the Royal Society originated. The initial issues of Oldenburg's journal were more like a newsletter, with descriptions of recent experiments and discoveries. For example, volume 1, issue 1, described the first-ever observation of what was probably the great red spot of Jupiter by the natural philosopher and polymath Robert Hook. The entire entry read, the ingenious Mr. Hook did some months since intimate to a friend of his that he had, with an excellent 12-foot telescope, observed some days before, he then spoke of it, Videl on 9 May 1664, about 9 of the clock at night. A small spot in the biggest of the three obscure belts of Jupiter and that observing it from time to time, he found that within two hours after the said spot had moved from east to west, about half the length of the diameter of Jupiter. The journal still exists to this day, with the somewhat easier to remember a title of philosophical transactions of the Royal Society. As time went on, the brief news items were replaced with longer articles containing detailed descriptions of experiments and studies. It's now part of a global ecosystem of over 30,000 journals ranging from the very general, like the highly prestigious journals Nature and Science, which aim to publish the world's most noteworthy research from any scientific field, to the very specific, like the American Journal of Potato Research, which is only interested in papers about one tuberous topic in particular. Some journals, like philosophical transactions, are still run by scientific societies, but most are owned by commercial outfits such as Elsevier, Wiley and Springer Nature. A recent advancement is that scientific journals are all online, allowing anyone who could afford to pay the publisher's subscription fees, or have their university library do so on their behalf, to have the world's scientific knowledge at their fingertips. After reading the journals relevant to your field, you might elite on a research question. Maybe there's a scientific theory that makes a prediction, an hypothesis, that you can test in some clever way. Maybe there's a gap in our existing knowledge that you know just how to plug. Maybe you've had a spark of inspiration and have come up with an experiment that tests something entirely new. Before you can do any of this though, you'll normally need some money to fund the study, for instance, to buy new equipment or materials, to recruit participants, or to pay the salaries of the scientists you'll hire to do the legwork. Unless you happen to be, say, a pharmaceutical company that can afford to run its own laboratories, the main way to get that all-important funding is to apply for a grant. This might come from your government, a business, an endowment fund, a nonprofit, a charity, or even a wealthy individual. You might apply to the National Institutes of Health, or the National Science Foundation, both of which are taxpayer-funded agencies in the United States, or to a science-funding charity like the Welcome Trust, or the Bill and Melinda Gates Foundation. Funding is by no means assured, and any scientists will tell you that one of the most grueling parts of the job is trying to get their latest research ideas funded with failure grindingly common. This grasping for cash has important knock-on effects on the science itself, and we'll return to them later in the book. But for now, let's imagine that you're successful in securing a grant. You can then get to work. Collecting the data might involve smashing particles together in an underground collider, finding fossils in the rocks of the Canadian Arctic, setting up the precise environment for bacterial growth in a petri dish, organizing hundreds of people to come to a lab and fill in questionnaires, or running a complex computer model. It can take days, months, decades. Once the data are in, you'll normally have a set of numbers that you, or a more mathematically minded colleague, can analyze using some variety of statistics, another minefield to which we'll return. Then you need to write it all up in the form of a scientific paper. The typical paper starts with an introduction, where you summarize what's known on the topic, and what your study adds. There follows a method section, where you describe exactly what you did, in enough detail, so that anyone could, in theory, run exactly the same experiment again. You'll then move on to a results section, where you present the numbers, tables, graphs, and statistical analyses that document your findings. And you land with a discussion section, where you speculate wildly, I mean, provide thoughtful informed consideration about what it all means. You'll top the whole thing with an abstract, a brief statement, usually of around 150 words, that summarizes the whole study and its results. The abstract is always available for anyone to read, even if the full paper is behind the journal's subscription paywall, so you'll want to use it to make your results sound compelling. Papers come in all lengths and sizes, and sometimes mix up the order I've described, but in general, your paper will end up along these lines. When the paper is ready, you enter the world of scientific journals, and the competition for publication. Until recently, submitting a paper to a journal meant printing out several hard copies and mailing them to the editor. But nowadays, everything is handled online. Though many journals still use such archaic, buggy websites that you might as well send your paper by carrier pigeon. The journal's editor, often a senior academic, will read the paper, or let's be honest, probably just the abstract, and decide whether it might be worth publishing. Most journals, especially the highly prestigious ones, pride themselves on their exclusivity and thus their low acceptance rate. Science, for example, accepts less than 7% of submissions. So the majority of papers will be bounced back to the authors at this point in what's called a desk rejection. This is the initial step in quality control, assorting by the editor of the papers into those that match the theme of the journal and have potential in terms of their scientific interest or quality, and those that aren't worth a second look. For the fraction of articles that do take the editor's fancy, now comes the moment of peer review. The editor will find two or three scientists who are experts in your field of research and ask them whether they'd like to evaluate your manuscript. They'll probably decline because they're too busy, so the editor will keep going down the list of possible reviewers until a few agree, and so begins the nail-biting wait to see if your work will receive their endorsement. Most people, including scientists, assume peer review has always been a crucial feature of scientific publication, but its history is more complicated. Although in the 17th century, the Royal Society tended to ask some of its members whether they thought a paper was interesting enough to publish in philosophical transactions, requiring them to provide a written evaluation of each study wasn't tried until at least 1831. Even then, the formal peer review system we know today didn't become universal until well into the 20th century. As you can tell from a letter Albert Einstein sent in 1936 to the editors of physical review, halffully announcing that he was withdrawing his paper from consideration at their journal because they had dared to send it to another physicist for comment. It took until the 1970s for all journals to adopt the modern model of sending out submissions to independent experts for peer review, giving them the gatekeeping role they have today. Peer reviewers are usually anonymous, which is both a blessing and a curse. A blessing because it allows them to speak their minds without concern about repercussions from the scientists whose work they're criticizing. And junior scientists can be truly honest about the flaws of a big name professors work, but a curse because, well, it allows them to speak their minds without concern about repercussions from the scientists whose work they're criticizing. The following are genuine excerpts from peer reviews. Some papers are a pleasure to read. This is not one of them. The results are as weak as a wet noodle. The manuscript makes three claims. The first we've known for years, the second for decades, the third for centuries. I am afraid this manuscript may contribute not so much towards the field advancement, as much as towards its eventual demise. Did you have a seizure while writing this sentence? Because I feel like I had one while reading it. If the reviewers' evaluations look like this, the editor will probably reject your paper. At that point, you might want to give up or start the whole process again by submitting to a different journal, and if that fails, a different one, and if that fails, a different one, and so on. It's not uncommon for papers to go through half a dozen or more journals, usually of ever-lower prestige, before they get accepted for publication. If, on the other hand, the reviewers are more impressed, you might get the opportunity to revise your paper to respond to their critiques, perhaps running new analyses or new experiments, or rewriting certain sections, and submit it to the editor again. The back and forth revising process can go through multiple rounds and often takes months. Eventually, if the reviewers are satisfied, the editor gives the go ahead and the paper is published. If the journal still prints hard copies, you'll get to see your precious work in print. Otherwise, you'll have to settle for the thrill of seeing it on the journal's official website. That's it. You've made your mark on the scientific literature, and you have a publication that you can add to your CV, and that can be cited by other researchers. Congratulations. Take the rest of the day off. That summary was all too brief in general, but essentially every scientific field follows that process in some form. We might ask ourselves whether, after being put through the mango of peer review, the eventual publication still provides a faithful representation of what was done in the study. We'll get to that in later chapters. For now, we need to consider something else. What ensures that the participants in the process just described, the researcher who submits the paper, the editor, the journal, the peers who review it, all conduct themselves with the honesty and integrity that trustworthy science requires. There's no law requiring that everyone acts fairly and rationally when evaluating science. So what's needed is a shared ethos, a set of values that aligns the scientist's behavior. The best known attempt to write down these unwritten rules is that of the sociologist Robert Merton. In 1942, Merton set out four scientific values, now known as the Mertonian norms. None of them have snappy names, but all of them are good aspirations for scientists. First, universalism. Scientific knowledge is scientific knowledge, no matter who comes up with it, so long as their methods for finding that knowledge are sound. The race, sex, age, gender, sexuality, income, social background, nationality, popularity, or any other status of a scientist should have no bearing on how their factual claims are assessed.
